</ul>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coffre_et_rouleau_de_Torah_ayant_appartenu_%C3%A0_Abraham_de_Camondo_chef_de_la_communaut%C3%A9_juive_de_Constantinople_1860_-_Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Art_et_d%27Histoire_du_Juda%C3%AFsme.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Coffre_et_rouleau_de_Torah_ayant_appartenu_%C3%A0_Abraham_de_Camondo_chef_de_la_communaut%C3%A9_juive_de_Constantinople_1860_-_Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Art_et_d%27Histoire_du_Juda%C3%AFsme.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" width="220" height="406" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Coffre_et_rouleau_de_Torah_ayant_appartenu_%C3%A0_Abraham_de_Camondo_chef_de_la_communaut%C3%A9_juive_de_Constantinople_1860_-_Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Art_et_d%27Histoire_du_Juda%C3%AFsme.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Coffre_et_rouleau_de_Torah_ayant_appartenu_%C3%A0_Abraham_de_Camondo_chef_de_la_communaut%C3%A9_juive_de_Constantinople_1860_-_Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Art_et_d%27Histoire_du_Juda%C3%AFsme.jpg 2x" data-file-width="433" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coffre_et_rouleau_de_Torah_ayant_appartenu_%C3%A0_Abraham_de_Camondo_chef_de_la_communaut%C3%A9_juive_de_Constantinople_1860_-_Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Art_et_d%27Histoire_du_Juda%C3%AFsme.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver case containing a handwritten <a href="/wiki/Sefer_Torah" title="Sefer Torah">Torah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Art_et_d%27Histoire_du_Juda%C3%AFsme" title="Musée d'Art et d'Histoire du Judaïsme">Museum of Jewish Art and History</a>, Paris)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Judaism</b> (originally from <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> <span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Shofar, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', Cardo, 'Noto Sans Hebrew', David, FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;" dir="rtl">יהודה</span>‎, <i>Yehudah</i>, "<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a>";<sup id="cite_ref-bibleinterp_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibleinterp-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-askoxford_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-askoxford-2">[2]</a></sup> via <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>) is an ancient <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Abrahamic religion">Abrahamic religion</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> as its foundational text.<sup id="cite_ref-uncertainties_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uncertainties-3">[3]</a></sup> It encompasses the <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Judaism_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judaism-4">[4]</a></sup> Judaism is considered by religious Jews to be the expression of the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_covenant" title="Mosaic covenant">covenant</a> that <a href="/wiki/God_in_Judaism" title="God in Judaism">God</a> established with the <a href="/wiki/Children_of_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Children of Israel">Children of Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Knowledge_Resources:_Judaism_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knowledge_Resources:_Judaism-5">[5]</a></sup> Judaism includes a wide corpus of texts, practices, theological positions, and forms of organization. The Torah is part of the larger text known as the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, and supplemental oral tradition represented by later texts such as the <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>. With between 14.5 and 17.4 million adherents worldwide,<sup id="cite_ref-Population_of_Jews_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population_of_Jews-6">[6]</a></sup> Judaism is <a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups#Religious_demographics" title="Major religious groups">the tenth-largest religion in the world</a>.</p>
<p>Within Judaism there are a variety of movements, most of which emerged from <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a>, which holds that God revealed his laws and <a href="/wiki/613_Mitzvot" class="mw-redirect" title="613 Mitzvot">commandments</a> to <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> on <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Mount_Sinai" title="Biblical Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a> in the form of both the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Written</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">Oral Torah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-What_is_the_oral_Torah.3F_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_is_the_oral_Torah.3F-7">[7]</a></sup> Historically, this assertion was challenged by various groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Sadducees#General" title="Sadducees">Sadducees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_period" title="Second Temple period">Second Temple period</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Karaite_Judaism#Karaite_interpretations_of_the_Torah" title="Karaite Judaism">Karaites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sabbateans" title="Sabbateans">Sabbateans</a> during the early and later medieval period;<sup id="cite_ref-Karaite_Jewish_University_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karaite_Jewish_University-8">[8]</a></sup> and among segments of the modern non-Orthodox denominations. Modern branches of Judaism such as <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_Judaism" title="Humanistic Judaism">Humanistic Judaism</a> may be <a href="/wiki/Nontheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Nontheistic">nontheistic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Society_for_Humanistic_Judaism_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Society_for_Humanistic_Judaism-9">[9]</a></sup> Today, the largest <a href="/wiki/Jewish_religious_movements" title="Jewish religious movements">Jewish religious movements</a> are <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haredi_Judaism" title="Haredi Judaism">Haredi Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Orthodox_Judaism" title="Modern Orthodox Judaism">Modern Orthodox Judaism</a>), <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">Conservative Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a>. Major sources of difference between these groups are their approaches to <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Jewish law</a>, the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbinic tradition">Rabbinic tradition</a>, and the significance of the <a href="/wiki/State_of_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="State of Israel">State of Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jewish_Denominations_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jewish_Denominations-10">[10]</a></sup> Orthodox Judaism maintains that the Torah and Jewish law are divine in origin, eternal and unalterable, and that they should be strictly followed. Conservative and Reform Judaism are more liberal, with Conservative Judaism generally promoting a more "traditional" interpretation of Judaism's requirements than Reform Judaism. A typical Reform position is that Jewish law should be viewed as a set of general guidelines rather than as a set of restrictions and obligations whose observance is required of all Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-Reform_Judaism_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reform_Judaism-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-What_is_Reform_Judaism.3F_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_is_Reform_Judaism.3F-12">[12]</a></sup> Historically, <a href="/wiki/Beth_din" title="Beth din">special courts</a> enforced Jewish law; today, these courts still exist but the practice of Judaism is mostly voluntary.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_Online_Encyclopedia:_Bet_Din_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_Online_Encyclopedia:_Bet_Din-13">[13]</a></sup> Authority on theological and legal matters is not vested in any one person or organization, but in the sacred texts and <a href="/wiki/Rabbis" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbis">rabbis</a> and scholars who interpret them.<sup id="cite_ref-religiousleadership_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-religiousleadership-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Judaism">history of Judaism</a> spans more than 3,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-MINDELL2009_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MINDELL2009-15">[15]</a></sup> Judaism has its roots as a structured religion in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Judaism is considered one of the oldest monotheistic religions.<sup id="cite_ref-Religion_.26_Ethics_.E2.80.93_Judaism_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Religion_.26_Ethics_.E2.80.93_Judaism-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">Hebrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> were already referred to as "Jews" in later books of the Tanakh such as the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Book of Esther</a>, with the term Jews replacing the title "Children of Israel".<sup id="cite_ref-google_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-19">[19]</a></sup> Judaism's texts, traditions and values strongly influenced later <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religions</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baha%27i_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Baha'i Faith">Baha'i Faith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Islam.2C_Judaism.2C_and_Christianity:_Theological_and_Historical_Affiliations_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Islam.2C_Judaism.2C_and_Christianity:_Theological_and_Historical_Affiliations-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Historical_Muhammad_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Historical_Muhammad-21">[21]</a></sup> Many aspects of Judaism have also directly or indirectly influenced secular Western <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> and civil law.<sup id="cite_ref-questia_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-questia-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Hebraism" title="Hebraism">Hebraism</a> is just as important a factor in the development of <a href="/wiki/Western_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a> as <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Hellenism" class="extiw" title="wikt:Hellenism">Hellenism</a>, and Judaism, as the mother religion of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, has considerably shaped Western ideals and morality since the Christian Era.<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge_University_Historical_Series_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_University_Historical_Series-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jews are an <a href="/wiki/Ethnoreligious_group" title="Ethnoreligious group">ethnoreligious group</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ethnoreligious_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnoreligious-24">[24]</a></sup> and include those born Jewish and <a href="/wiki/Converts_to_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Converts to Judaism">converts to Judaism</a>. In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_population_by_country" title="Jewish population by country">world Jewish population</a> was estimated at about 14.3&#160;million, or roughly 0.2% of the total world population.<sup id="cite_ref-WJP2015_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WJP2015-25">[25]</a></sup> About 43% of all Jews reside in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and another 43% reside in the United States and Canada, with most of the remainder living in Europe, and other minority groups spread throughout South America, Asia, Africa, and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-WJP2015_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WJP2015-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_characteristics_and_principles_of_faith">Defining characteristics and principles of faith</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_characteristics">Defining characteristics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:5492_-_Venezia_-_Ghetto_Nuovo_-_Negozio_ebraico_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto,_1-Aug-2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/5492_-_Venezia_-_Ghetto_Nuovo_-_Negozio_ebraico_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_1-Aug-2008.jpg/250px-5492_-_Venezia_-_Ghetto_Nuovo_-_Negozio_ebraico_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_1-Aug-2008.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/5492_-_Venezia_-_Ghetto_Nuovo_-_Negozio_ebraico_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_1-Aug-2008.jpg/375px-5492_-_Venezia_-_Ghetto_Nuovo_-_Negozio_ebraico_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_1-Aug-2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/5492_-_Venezia_-_Ghetto_Nuovo_-_Negozio_ebraico_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_1-Aug-2008.jpg/500px-5492_-_Venezia_-_Ghetto_Nuovo_-_Negozio_ebraico_-_Foto_Giovanni_Dall%27Orto%2C_1-Aug-2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1224" /></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg/250px-%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg" width="250" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg/375px-%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg/500px-%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3400" data-file-height="2432" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D1%85%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0_-_%D7%9E%D7%A7%D7%93%D7%95%D7%A0%D7%99_%D7%97%D7%A0%D7%95%D7%9B%D7%99%D7%99%D7%94_-_Macedonian_Hanukkah_menorah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 19th-century silver <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Macedonian</a> Hanukkah menorah</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike other ancient Near Eastern gods, the Hebrew God is portrayed as unitary and solitary; consequently, the Hebrew God's principal relationships are not with other gods, but with the world, and more specifically, with the people he created.<sup id="cite_ref-understanding_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-understanding-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Judaism thus begins with ethical monotheism: the belief that God is one and is concerned with the actions of mankind.<sup id="cite_ref-google1_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-27">[27]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> (Hebrew Bible), God promised <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a> to make of his offspring a great nation.<sup id="cite_ref-everlasting_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-everlasting-28">[28]</a></sup> Many generations later, he commanded the nation of <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israel</a> to love and worship only one God; that is, the Jewish nation is to reciprocate God's concern for the world.<sup id="cite_ref-deuteronomy_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deuteronomy-29">[29]</a></sup> He also commanded the Jewish people to love one another; that is, Jews are to imitate God's love for people.<sup id="cite_ref-leviticus_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leviticus-30">[30]</a></sup> These commandments are but two of a large corpus of <a href="/wiki/613_mitzvot" class="mw-redirect" title="613 mitzvot">commandments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">laws</a> that constitute this <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(biblical)" title="Covenant (biblical)">covenant</a>, which is the substance of Judaism.</p>
<p>Thus, although there is an esoteric tradition in Judaism (<a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>), Rabbinic scholar <a href="/wiki/Max_Kadushin" title="Max Kadushin">Max Kadushin</a> has characterized normative Judaism as "normal mysticism", because it involves everyday personal experiences of God through ways or modes that are common to all Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-publishing_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-publishing-31">[31]</a></sup> This is played out through the observance of the <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakha</a> (Jewish law) and given verbal expression in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Jewish_prayers_and_blessings" title="List of Jewish prayers and blessings">Birkat Ha-Mizvot</a>, the short blessings that are spoken every time a positive commandment is to be fulfilled.</p>
<dl>
<dd>The ordinary, familiar, everyday things and occurrences we have, constitute occasions for the experience of God. Such things as one's daily sustenance, the very day itself, are felt as manifestations of God's loving-kindness, calling for the <i>Berakhot</i>. <i>Kedushah</i>, holiness, which is nothing else than the imitation of God, is concerned with daily conduct, with being gracious and merciful, with keeping oneself from defilement by idolatry, adultery, and the shedding of blood. The <i>Birkat Ha-Mitzwot</i> evokes the consciousness of holiness at a rabbinic rite, but the objects employed in the majority of these rites are non-holy and of general character, while the several holy objects are <a href="/wiki/Theurgy" title="Theurgy">non-theurgic.</a> And not only do ordinary things and occurrences bring with them the experience of God. Everything that happens to a man evokes that experience, evil as well as good, for a <i>Berakah</i> is said also at evil tidings. Hence, although the experience of God is like none other, the <i>occasions</i> for experiencing Him, for having a consciousness of Him, are manifold, even if we consider only those that call for Berakot.<sup id="cite_ref-publishing2_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-publishing2-32">[32]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Whereas <a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy" title="Jewish philosophy">Jewish philosophers</a> often debate whether God is <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanent</a> or <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendent</a>, and whether people have free will or their lives are determined, <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakha</a> is a system through which any Jew acts to bring God into the world.</p>
<p>Ethical monotheism is central in all sacred or normative texts of Judaism. However, monotheism has not always been followed in practice. The Jewish Bible (<a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>) records and repeatedly condemns the widespread worship of other gods in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Israel">ancient Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mechon-mamre_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mechon-mamre-33">[33]</a></sup> In the Greco-Roman era, many different interpretations of monotheism existed in Judaism, including the interpretations that gave rise to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Jewish_roots_of_Christological_monotheism:_papers_from_the_St._Andrews_conference_on_the_historical_origins_of_the_worship_of_Jesus_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Jewish_roots_of_Christological_monotheism:_papers_from_the_St._Andrews_conference_on_the_historical_origins_of_the_worship_of_Jesus-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moreover, some have argued that Judaism is a non-creedal religion that does not require one to believe in God.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> For some, observance of Jewish law is more important than belief in God <i>per se</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-jovanovich_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jovanovich-35">[35]</a></sup> In modern times, some liberal Jewish movements do not accept the existence of a personified deity active in history.<sup id="cite_ref-movements_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-movements-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The debate about whether one can speak of authentic or normative Judaism is not only a debate among religious Jews but also among historians.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_tenets">Core tenets</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_principles_of_faith" title="Jewish principles of faith">Jewish principles of faith</a></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:35%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>13 Principles of Faith:
<ol>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the Creator, Blessed be His Name, is the Creator and Guide of everything that has been created; He alone has made, does make, and will make all things.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the Creator, Blessed be His Name, is One, and that there is no unity in any manner like His, and that He alone is our God, who was, and is, and will be.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the Creator, Blessed be His Name, has no body, and that He is free from all the properties of matter, and that there can be no (physical) comparison to Him whatsoever.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the Creator, Blessed be His Name, is the first and the last.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that to the Creator, Blessed be His Name, and to Him alone, it is right to pray, and that it is not right to pray to any being besides Him.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that all the words of the prophets are true.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the prophecy of Moses our teacher, <a href="/wiki/Honorifics_for_the_dead_in_Judaism#Peace_be_upon_him.2Fher" title="Honorifics for the dead in Judaism">peace be upon him</a>, was true, and that he was the chief of the prophets, both those who preceded him and those who followed him.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the entire Torah that is now in our possession is the same that was given to Moses our teacher, peace be upon him.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that this Torah will not be exchanged, and that there will never be any other Torah from the Creator, Blessed be His Name.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the Creator, Blessed be His Name, knows all the deeds of human beings and all their thoughts, as it is written, "Who fashioned the hearts of them all, Who comprehends all their actions" (<a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a> 33:15).</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that the Creator, Blessed be His Name, rewards those who keep His commandments and punishes those that transgress them.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith in the coming of the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_messianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish messianism">Messiah</a>; and even though he may tarry, nonetheless, I wait every day for his coming.</li>
<li>I believe with perfect faith that there will be a revival of the dead at the time when it shall please the Creator, Blessed be His name, and His mention shall be exalted for ever and ever.</li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a></cite></div>
<p>Scholars throughout <a href="/wiki/Jewish_history" title="Jewish history">Jewish history</a> have proposed numerous formulations of Judaism's core tenets, all of which have met with criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-montpelier_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-montpelier-39">[39]</a></sup> The most popular formulation is <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>' <a href="/wiki/13_principles_of_faith" class="mw-redirect" title="13 principles of faith">thirteen principles of faith</a>, developed in the 12th century. According to Maimonides, any Jew who rejects even one of these principles would be considered an apostate and a heretic.<sup id="cite_ref-mesora_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mesora-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maimonides.2C_13_Principles_of_Faith_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maimonides.2C_13_Principles_of_Faith-41">[41]</a></sup> Jewish scholars have held points of view diverging in various ways from Maimonides' principles.<sup id="cite_ref-learning_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-learning-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_JPS_guide_to_Jewish_traditions_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_JPS_guide_to_Jewish_traditions-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Maimonides' time, his list of tenets was criticized by <a href="/wiki/Hasdai_Crescas" title="Hasdai Crescas">Hasdai Crescas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Albo" title="Joseph Albo">Joseph Albo</a>. Albo and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_ben_David" title="Abraham ben David">the Raavad</a> argued that Maimonides' principles contained too many items that, while true, were not fundamentals of the faith.</p>
<p>Along these lines, the ancient historian <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> emphasized practices and observances rather than religious beliefs, associating <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a> with a failure to observe Jewish law and maintaining that the requirements for conversion to Judaism included <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">circumcision</a> and adherence to traditional customs. Maimonides' principles were largely ignored over the next few centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-medieval_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medieval-44">[44]</a></sup> Later, two poetic restatements of these principles ("<i><a href="/wiki/Ani_Ma%27amin" title="Ani Ma'amin">Ani Ma'amin</a></i>" and "<i><a href="/wiki/Yigdal" title="Yigdal">Yigdal</a></i>") became integrated into many Jewish liturgies,<sup id="cite_ref-The_Thirteen_Principles_of_the_Jewish_Faith_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Thirteen_Principles_of_the_Jewish_Faith-45">[45]</a></sup> leading to their eventual near-universal acceptance.<sup id="cite_ref-What_Do_Jews_Believe.3F_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What_Do_Jews_Believe.3F-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-traditions_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-traditions-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In modern times, Judaism lacks a centralized authority that would dictate an exact religious dogma.<sup id="cite_ref-religiousleadership_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-religiousleadership-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-judaism101beliefs_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-judaism101beliefs-48">[48]</a></sup> Because of this, many different variations on the basic beliefs are considered within the scope of Judaism.<sup id="cite_ref-learning_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-learning-42">[42]</a></sup> Even so, all <a href="/wiki/Jewish_religious_movements" title="Jewish religious movements">Jewish religious movements</a> are, to a greater or lesser extent, based on the principles of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> and various commentaries such as the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a>. Judaism also universally recognizes the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(biblical)" title="Covenant (biblical)">Covenant</a> between God and the <a href="/wiki/Patriarchs_(Bible)" title="Patriarchs (Bible)">Patriarch</a> <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a> as well as the additional aspects of the Covenant revealed to <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, who is considered Judaism's greatest <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-learning_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-learning-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ontario_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ontario-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-patriarchscovenant_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patriarchscovenant-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-How_Do_You_Know_the_Exodus_Really_Happened.3F_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Do_You_Know_the_Exodus_Really_Happened.3F-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sacredtexts_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sacredtexts-52">[52]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a>, a core text of <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a>, acceptance of the Divine origins of this covenant is considered an essential aspect of Judaism and those who reject the Covenant forfeit their share in the <a href="/wiki/World_to_Come" class="mw-redirect" title="World to Come">World to Come</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sacred-texts_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sacred-texts-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Establishing the core tenets of Judaism in the modern era is even more difficult, given the number and diversity of the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Jewish_religious_movements" title="Jewish religious movements">Jewish denominations</a>. Even if to restrict the problem to the most influential intellectual trends of the nineteenth and twentieth century, the matter remains complicated. Thus for instance, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_B._Soloveitchik" title="Joseph B. Soloveitchik">Joseph Soloveitchik's</a> (associated with the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Orthodox_Judaism" title="Modern Orthodox Judaism">Modern Orthodox movement</a>) answer to modernity is constituted upon the identification of Judaism with following the <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakha</a> whereas its ultimate goal is to bring the holiness down to the world. <a href="/wiki/Mordecai_Kaplan" title="Mordecai Kaplan">Mordecai Kaplan</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Reconstructionist_Judaism" title="Reconstructionist Judaism">Reconstructionist Judaism</a>, abandons the idea of religion for the sake of identifying Judaism with <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> and by means of the latter term and secular translation of the core ideas, he tries to embrace as many Jewish denominations as possible. In turn, <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Schechter" title="Solomon Schechter">Solomon Schechter</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">Conservative Judaism</a> was identical with the tradition understood as the interpretation of Torah, in itself being the history of the constant updates and adjustment of the Law performed by means of the creative interpretation. Finally, <a href="/wiki/David_Philipson" title="David Philipson">David Philipson</a> draws the outlines of the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform movement</a> in Judaism by opposing it to the strict and traditional rabbinical approach and thus comes to the conclusions similar to that of the Conservative movement.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_religious_texts">Jewish religious texts</span></h2>
